LANG_ENG The Turkish press at the time complained mainly of the failure of German policy following the attacks . Helmut Kohl declined to visit the survivors in Mölln .
LANG_ENG He sent a telegram of commiseration to the Turkish president after the arson attack in Solingen , but it was his foreign minister , Klaus Kinkel , who took his place at the memorial ceremony in Cologne - Kinkel summed up in his speech the taxes Turks contribute in Germany ,
LANG_ENG just as if he were calculating the value of a person by the taxes he pays .
LANG_ENG The coverage by the Turkish media at the time again shows how deep the mistrust of many German Turks in the current German state has become since .
LANG_ENG Straight talking is what is needed now , alongside efforts to identify and reassess right-wing extremist terrorism in Germany .
LANG_ENG The Lake Zurich banking scandal
LANG_ENG The most risk-taking bank in Europe is not in Italy or France , but in Switzerland .
LANG_ENG UBS almost went bankrupt during the financial crisis and has been struggling from one crisis to the next ever since .
LANG_ENG A German is now to bring it back in line .
LANG_ENG Axel Weber certainly had not imagined that would be his role .
LANG_ENG After his spectacular departure from the head of the Federal bank , it was announced in July that he would be starting an almost equally spectacular new job in 2013: Chairman of the Board of Directors of the major Swiss bank UBS - an unexpected and lucrative career move .
LANG_ENG Axel Weber was clearly delighted , praising Switzerland for its beauty and UBS for its newly won solidity and making some friendly remarks about his future colleagues .
LANG_ENG &quot; UBS has managed to turn around . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; I am looking forward to working with Kaspar Villiger and Oswald Grübel . &quot;
LANG_ENG But by September it was already clear that everything would be very different .
LANG_ENG A UBS trader in London had bypassed all the bank &apos;s security systems and gambled away around 1.7 billion Euros .
LANG_ENG UBS CEO , Oswald Grübel - a banker not only famous in Switzerland - had to go .
LANG_ENG The UBS Chairman has been considered ailing ever since - and everyone is asking: when will Axel Weber finally arrive ?
LANG_ENG Weber is to advise the bank from February 2012 .
LANG_ENG And he will take on the role of Chairman of the Board in May , and not in 2013 , the bank announced on Tuesday .
LANG_ENG That will be tough for Axel Weber .
LANG_ENG He is an economics professor and central banker , not a conventional banker , and clearly would need some time to adjust .
LANG_ENG All at once , he is taking on responsibility for the strategy of a bank that has once again proven that it has long deserved the title of &quot; Europe &apos;s biggest banking scandal . &quot;
LANG_ENG UBS is both huge - it was the biggest asset manager in the world until recently - and has been struggling from one scandal to the next for years .
LANG_ENG The proud Swiss bank , it seems , is to be spared nothing .
LANG_ENG It began in the financial crisis .
LANG_ENG UBS had an excellent reputation until 2007 .
LANG_ENG The bank combined the image of the secretive and solid , but equally savvy asset manager with ambitious targets , aiming to become a major player in investment banking .
LANG_ENG As a result , in 2007 UBS was managing 1.6 billion , thereby managing more more money from wealthy clients than any other bank in the world ,
LANG_ENG and had risen to the league of the world &apos;s biggest banks as an investment bank .
LANG_ENG That made an impression .
LANG_ENG The former banker and long-standing banking professor at the Swiss Institute of Banking of the University of Zurich , Hans Geiger , explains: &quot; I always presented UBS to my students as the model of a good bank . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; From what we know today , you have to ask how I could be so wrong . &quot;
LANG_ENG He was very wrong indeed .
LANG_ENG It only became clear in 2007 that something was really going very wrong at UBS .
LANG_ENG In the middle of the year , CEO Peter Wuffli - considered a highly intellectual and equally brilliant banker - suddenly stepped down .
LANG_ENG Some months later we could guess at why he had left .
LANG_ENG The investment division of the bank had gambled on subprime securities and was forced to write off billions .
LANG_ENG UBS announced a capital increase , believe it or not , of 13 billion Swiss francs to face the crisis .
LANG_ENG The bank actually managed to find investors , but investors many bankers would have respectfully declined a short while earlier - a less than transparent Asian sovereign wealth fund from Singapore and an Arabic sovereign wealth fund that was so secretive , even its name was not disclosed .
LANG_ENG That was just the first blow .
LANG_ENG It got a lot worse in April 2008 .
LANG_ENG The bank was forced to announce new losses running into the billions and Chairman of the Board of Directors , Marcel Ospel - the second best paid chairman in Switzerland in 2006 - resigned .
LANG_ENG Catastrophe followed in October 2008 . UBS faced collapse from a lack of investment during the financial crisis and had to be saved by the state .
LANG_ENG The National Bank bought up toxic securities from UBS for untold billions and the Swiss Confederation paid six billion Swiss francs for a mandatory convertible bond to become part owner of the bank .
LANG_ENG The whole of Switzerland was shocked - the country recognised the dramatic consequences for a small country of being home to such large banks .
LANG_ENG After all , the balance sheet total for UBS in 2007 was more than five times the economic output of Switzerland (see chart).
LANG_ENG &quot; We would not be able to save the UBS for a second time , &quot; says banking researcher Geiger .
LANG_ENG The second catastrophe followed closely on the heels of the first .
LANG_ENG A few months later , UBS capitulated in the tax dispute with the USA and declared that it was prepared to hand over the data of wealthy clients who had potentially avoided paying tax with the help of the bank .
LANG_ENG UBS also agreed to pay 780 million dollars consisting of repayment of unjustified profits from the business and a penalty .
LANG_ENG UBS therefore more or less admitted having been an accessory to tax evasion .
LANG_ENG At the same time , UBS lost something that had always been its advantage when competing for rich clients - the fog of secrecy .
LANG_ENG Swiss banking secrecy was not the same after the scandal .
LANG_ENG The CEO and the Chairman then resigned and
LANG_ENG the exodus of client money began .
LANG_ENG The bank had lost its credibility in both of its core business divisions within a few months - in investment banking thanks to bad speculation and in asset management by aiding and abetting tax evasion .
LANG_ENG And just as things had calmed down and UBS looked like it would start to turn things around , along came a disastrous rogue trader . A single trader gambled away 1.7 billion Euros before the bank noticed something was wrong .
LANG_ENG One thing is clear - risk management in the investment banking division is still not working .
LANG_ENG &quot; The Adoboli incident clearly showed that risk control had changed too little , &quot; says Martin Janssen , economic and professor of finance at the Swiss Banking Institute of the University of Zurich .
LANG_ENG The CFO of one major German bank also has no doubts: &quot; The problem facing UBS is that it does not having a handle on risk control . &quot;
LANG_ENG It was precisely the concept of caution that had long ago made Swiss bankers the guardians of the world &apos;s major assets - no excessive risk , but security for money , whether the world fell apart outside the Alpine republic or not .
LANG_ENG The safes of Swiss banks had become a symbol of unshakeable reliability , just as Lindt and Sprüngli had come to symbolise sophisticated chocolate .
LANG_ENG The UBS headquarters in Zurich still fit the cliché of the dignified Swiss private bank - just a bit bigger .
LANG_ENG The location alone is classic .
LANG_ENG The bank has its head office on Zurich &apos;s Bahnhofstrasse that leads from the main station directly to Zurich Lake , surrounded by the snow-capped peaks of the Swiss mountains .
LANG_ENG The buildings are ostentatious and the shop rates are among the highest in Europe .
LANG_ENG Jewellers such as Tiffany &apos;s are to be found here between traditional Swiss shops such as Confiserie Sprüngli .
LANG_ENG The Swiss banks have resided here for hundreds of years - from the National Bank to the palace of Credit Suisse and elite private banks such as Julius Bär .
LANG_ENG A heavy revolving door opens onto the UBS reception .
LANG_ENG The walls within are clad in marble behind vast leather armchairs . There is a large sign to the &quot; Safe &quot; next to the bank &apos;s logo .
LANG_ENG UBS knows that its clients are looking for - security .
LANG_ENG But both the major Swiss banks , UBS and Credit Suisse , at some point ousted the Swiss banking concept - or betrayed it , as the head of one small Swiss private bank believes .
LANG_ENG He would prefer not to see his name in the newspaper - he too , like all Swiss bankers , does business with the two major banks .
LANG_ENG He believes UBS has grown too big - particularly in investment banking where the Anglo-Saxons are also very active .
LANG_ENG In his opinion , the Swiss have nothing to add .
LANG_ENG &quot; And if they do , they have adapted to the customs of the Anglo-Saxons long ago . &quot;
LANG_ENG It was in fact Anglo-Saxon investment banking that changed UBS , brought in its first big profits - and then the debacle . No other European financial institution has lost as much money in the subprime jungle as the major Swiss bank .
LANG_ENG The catastrophe became evident on 15 October 2008 .
LANG_ENG &quot; That Wednesday , the government coalition of a small country with a population of seven million undertook the largest rescue operation in the history of the nation , &quot; writes author Lukas Hässig in his book The UBS Crash .
LANG_ENG The politicians approved up to 68 billion Swiss francs (around 45 billion Euros) to save the bank from bankruptcy .
LANG_ENG How could it have come to that ?
LANG_ENG Talking to Swiss banks , they see the issue mainly in the size of UBS .
LANG_ENG Two of the three major Swiss banks consolidated into a single , vast group in June 1998 .
LANG_ENG A bank was created that was already too big for small Switzerland when the merger was agreed - and a bank that continued to grow .
LANG_ENG The fact that the two banks that went to form UBS complemented each other helped . One was a wealthy but sedate bank specialising in asset management in Zurich and the other was less wealthy , but much more internationalised .
LANG_ENG The internationalised investment bankers suddenly had access to a lot of money that they could use to turn an almighty wheel .
LANG_ENG Two names are connected with the rise of UBS: Peter Wuffli , Group CEO of UBS from 2001 to 2007 , and Marcel Ospel , Chairman of the Board of Directors .
LANG_ENG &quot; I always thought that Wuffli and Ospel were a dream team , &quot; says the banking research , Hans Geiger .
LANG_ENG &quot; Wuffli , highly intellectual , modest , with a deep understanding of models and statistics , and Ospel , with gut instinct who had worked his way up from being a banker &apos;s apprentice . &quot;
LANG_ENG But Ospel in the end relied on Wuffli .
LANG_ENG And Wuffli ultimately may have made a critical error . &quot; He thought his models were reality , &quot; says Geiger .
LANG_ENG &quot; That is tragic . &quot;
LANG_ENG Tragic it may be .
LANG_ENG Pride comes before a fall .
LANG_ENG Employees of UBS clearly remember the management meetings where CEO Wuffli always repeated the same slogan - dethrone Goldman Sachs .
LANG_ENG The American bank was the world &apos;s most powerful investment bank .
LANG_ENG It led the &quot; league tables &quot; that documented the success of the banks at that time .
LANG_ENG UBS wanted to get to the very top of the league .
LANG_ENG It managed some of the way .
LANG_ENG UBS was a major player in foreign exchange and stocks .
LANG_ENG Commissioned management consultants , however , pointed out a weakness . In terms of what in banking terminology is referred to as &quot; fixed income , &quot; UBS was far behind the very largest banks .
LANG_ENG UBS had gaps in its US mortgage products .
LANG_ENG To plug the hole , UBS entered on an adventure that almost cost it its existence .
LANG_ENG Hungry for growth , the bank itself and a hedge fund founded by the bank invested in US subprime securities .
LANG_ENG It developed into a vast machine that packaged up securities based on US mortgage credit and passed them on - while itself continuing to bear risks .
LANG_ENG Even when other banks had long been looking for a way out , UBS carried on .
LANG_ENG &quot; UBS was buying as late as the summer of 2007 when the US housing market was in flames , &quot; says banking expert Geiger .
LANG_ENG The greater the rise , the greater the fall .
LANG_ENG One manager after the next had to go as the attempts to clean up proved more difficult than anticipated .
LANG_ENG Posts at UBS became a game of musical chairs and remain uncertain to this day .
LANG_ENG Any head of the major bank now has an almost impossible job .
LANG_ENG He must find a strategy for a bank that has turned its back on all existing business models .
LANG_ENG Banking expert Geiger already has an idea - quit investment banking , focus on asset management and turn back to Swiss roots .
LANG_ENG The bank , however , is not (yet) prepared to go that far .
LANG_ENG It is clear , nonetheless , that investment banking must shrink significantly .
LANG_ENG The new head of the bank , Sergio Ermotti , plans to present his future strategy to the world on Tuesday .
LANG_ENG The 51-year-old Italo-Swiss banker was actually only imagined as a transitional solution when the bank parted ways unexpectedly with his predecessor Grübel .
LANG_ENG Since then , he has proved his mettle and is now to head UBS permanently , the Zurich-based bank announced on Tuesday .
LANG_ENG Ermotti plans to take the investment bank back to where it was in the mid-90s .
LANG_ENG He is cutting 3500 jobs .
LANG_ENG But that will not be enough .
LANG_ENG UBS needs a vision .
LANG_ENG What is UBS ?
LANG_ENG How does it plan to make money ?
LANG_ENG Those are the questions Axel Weber is definitely already asking himself .
LANG_ENG Publicly , he has only so far that he and his wife will look for a flat in Zurich .
LANG_ENG Next year .

LANG_ENG Intelligent crows are spreading in cities
LANG_ENG Rooks are considered problem birds in many places . Experts now say the highly intelligent animals can no longer be driven from cities .
LANG_ENG Some like the birds , others would like to get rid of them as fast as possible . Crows have been part of the cityscape for hundreds of years , but residents complaining about the filth and noise the animals create are increasing in number in many communities .
LANG_ENG A solution to the problem birds , however , is far from within reach .
LANG_ENG Experts at the first crow symposium in the East Frisian town of Leer have said that rooks can no longer be forced out of cities .
LANG_ENG Specialists from all over Germany discussed possible solutions .
LANG_ENG Looking for alternatives in the cities
LANG_ENG Conservationists stated that migration of specially protected rooks was the root of the problem in cities , pointing out that the use of chemicals in agriculture was increasingly destroying traditional habitats .
LANG_ENG Hunting , increasing development and felling of typical breeding trees was making life hard for the birds .
LANG_ENG Crows are protected .
LANG_ENG There are large colonies with more than 600 breeding pairs in the Westphalian town of Soest and in the north-west in Jever , Diepholz , Achim bei Bremen and Leer .
LANG_ENG A controversial project to cull crows in Leer was behind negative headlines in 2005 .
LANG_ENG Critics described the capture of the birds in bird traps and how they were beaten to death with clubs as particularly brutal .
LANG_ENG &quot; Normal displacement and deterrence does nothing , &quot; said town and country planner Werner Klöver from Leer .
LANG_ENG Spraying off nests with water or felling trees often has undesired consequences , said environment expert Manfred Kaiser from Lahr in Baden-Württemberg .
LANG_ENG &quot; The animals disperse and large colonies split up and spread to other areas . &quot;
LANG_ENG Deterrence would only be conceivable as an exception in hospitals , retirement homes and schools .
LANG_ENG &quot; We have to live with them . Getting rid of them is not an option . &quot;
LANG_ENG Without the surrounding area , the problem in the cities will not be solved , said Leer &apos;s mayor , Wolfgang Kellner .
LANG_ENG A combination of deterring birds from sensitive residential areas and offering incentives for them to move back to other areas would be an option .
LANG_ENG &quot; Information boards instead of chain saws &quot; was the slogan proposed by the psychologist and ethnologist Uta Maria Jürgens , who argued for a totally different approach to the birds . &quot; We should have the self-confidence to live with this problem . &quot;
LANG_ENG Jürgens was a co-founder of the Ascherberg crow educational trail in Schleswig-Holstein , where notice boards provide information about the life of the highly intelligent rook .
LANG_ENG The project is already attracting interest from tourists .
LANG_ENG Jürgens also expressed her opinion on the criticism of the noise the birds make . &quot; The noise conveys a lot of information . They have a lot to say to each other . &quot;
LANG_ENG The intelligence and skill of the birds are legendary . Rooks often do not just use tools to get hold of a choice morsel , but create tools themselves .
LANG_ENG Behavioural researchers at the University of Cambridge recently tested the extent of the birds &quot; intelligence on four rooks .
LANG_ENG In one experiment , the birds quickly learnt that they had to knock over a platform to get to a moth larva .
LANG_ENG They recognised the size and shape of stone needed to collapse the platform without much training .
LANG_ENG In a second experiment , the canny crows had to solve a trickier problem .
LANG_ENG The researchers put a small bucket with a moth larva in a vertical cylinder .
LANG_ENG The birds could not reach their prey with their beaks .
LANG_ENG However , the researchers had provided pieces of wire .
LANG_ENG All four birds used the wire to form a hook and used the hook to fish the bucket out of the tube by its handle .
LANG_ENG Three of the four rooks even managed it at the first attempt .

LANG_ENG Mass Slaughter on a Personal Level
LANG_ENG Peter Englund &apos;s intense and bighearted new book , &quot; The Beauty and the Sorrow: An Intimate History of the First World War , &quot; begins with a long dramatis personae of the sort that can make your heart sink .
LANG_ENG Here is advice for proceeding: Gently excise this page and make it your bookmark .
LANG_ENG You will be getting to know these people very well in Mr. Englund &apos;s novelistic telling , and this dramatis personae will function as your GPS , a beacon during those few moments when , like one of his men and women , you are confused and bereft in the fog of war .
LANG_ENG Mr. Englund is a Swedish historian and journalist .
LANG_ENG He is also the new permanent secretary of the Swedish Academy , which awards the Nobel Prize in Literature .
LANG_ENG What he has written here is an unusual book , one he describes , not inaccurately , as &quot; a work of anti-history . &quot;
LANG_ENG It contains few big names , major treaties or famous battles; there are almost no ambassadors , dashing journalists or discussions of tactics and materiel .
LANG_ENG It &apos;s not so much a book about what happened , he explains , as &quot; a book about what it was like . &quot; It &apos;s about &quot; feelings , impressions , experiences and moods . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; The Beauty and the Sorrow &quot; threads together the wartime experiences of 20 more or less unremarkable men and women , on both sides of the war , from schoolgirls and botanists to mountain climbers , doctors , ambulance drivers and clerks .
LANG_ENG A few of these people will become heroes .
LANG_ENG A few will become prisoners of war , or lose limbs , go mad or die .
LANG_ENG Their lives flicker here like votive candles lighted in a church; new ones are added to the mix while some wink out .
LANG_ENG Mr. Englund &apos;s book is a deviation from standard history books .
LANG_ENG It is a corrective too to the notion that World War I was only about the dire trench warfare on the Western Front .
LANG_ENG &quot; The Beauty and the Sorrow &quot; expertly pans across other theaters of war: the Alps , the Balkans , the Eastern Front , Mesopotamia , East Africa .
LANG_ENG Soldiers in this book have beehives fall on them; one has Christmas in Egypt under the pyramids; tsetse flies are an intractable problem .
LANG_ENG This is a moving book , almost from the start .
LANG_ENG War floods these people &apos;s lives like a natural catastrophe , a Hurricane Katrina that reeks of cordite .
LANG_ENG When cannon fire is heard in the distance , and you are a woman at home with your children , do you stay or flee ?
LANG_ENG Who is coming , anyway ?
LANG_ENG Almost no one understands what &apos;s happening , even why this war is to be fought .
LANG_ENG &quot; Lack of facts , &quot; Mr. Englund observes , &quot; has been padded out with guesses , suppositions , hopes , fears , idées fixes , conspiracy theories , dreams , nightmares and rumors . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; The Beauty and the Sorrow &quot; follows individuals like Florence Farmborough , an English nurse in the Russian Army , and Richard Stumpf , a young German high-seas fleet seaman .
LANG_ENG Their stories are mostly taken from memoirs , letters and other already published material .
LANG_ENG The accounts of their lives can be terrifying or stirring , but are most fully alive in Mr. Englund &apos;s accumulation of small moments , stray details .
LANG_ENG Many of these are about deprivation and making do .
LANG_ENG In telling a German schoolgirl &apos;s story he says: &quot; Ersatz , everywhere ersatz . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; Substitute coffee , fake aluminum , imitation rubber , paper bandages , wooden buttons . &quot;
LANG_ENG He goes on about the ersatz food in Germany .
LANG_ENG He describes &quot; meat made of pressed rice boiled in mutton fat (and finished off with a fake bone made of wood); tobacco made of dried roots and dried potato peel; shoes soled with wood . &quot;
LANG_ENG There are , he notes , &quot; 837 registered meat substitutes permissible in the production of sausages , 511 registered coffee substitutes . &quot;
LANG_ENG Some stories are about honor and bravery .
LANG_ENG One American recognizes his own impulse toward savagery and declares about war: &quot; You feel that , after all , this is what men were intended for rather than to sit in easy chairs with a cigarette and whiskey , the evening paper or the best seller , and to pretend that such a veneer means civilization and that there is no barbarian behind your starched and studded shirt front . &quot;
LANG_ENG Conversely a British soldier realizes that he will most likely die , and that no one will notice or care .
LANG_ENG &quot; Once one is resigned to the thought of sacrificing oneself , one would like to think that it might happen in front of an audience , &quot; he writes .
LANG_ENG Instead , it is horribly like &quot; a condemned man being strangled secretly . &quot;
LANG_ENG Other observations are about an old Europe falling away , and about new types of terror .
LANG_ENG &quot; The conflict has increasingly become an economic competition , &quot; Mr. Englund writes , &quot; a war between factories . &quot;
LANG_ENG He catches the arrival of what he calls &quot; a new species in the bestiary of the young century: the articulate and ideologically convinced mass murderer in well-cut clothes who performs his butchery while sitting behind a desk . &quot;
LANG_ENG People behave in unanticipated ways; there is as much base behavior as heroism .
LANG_ENG Mr. Englund discussed the soldiers who actively tried to catch a venereal disease from prostitutes as a way to evade service at the front .
LANG_ENG &quot; The most grotesque expression of this can be seen in the trade of gonococcal pus , which soldiers buy and smear into their genitals in the hope of ending up in hospital , &quot; he writes .
LANG_ENG &quot; Those who are really desperate rub it into their eyes , which often results in lifelong blindness . &quot;
LANG_ENG In this translation from the Swedish by Peter Graves , Mr. Englund &apos;s prose is supple but unshowy , perfectly suited to his humane task .
LANG_ENG In dozens of small scenes he catches the way the war has &quot; unleashed uniquely uncontrollable forces: extreme nationalism , social revolution , religious hatred . &quot;
LANG_ENG People begin to ask why their leaders are making them fight .
LANG_ENG The best books about World War I have often been oblique , like Paul Fussell &apos;s &quot; Great War and Modern Memory , &quot; or novels , like Erich Maria Remarque &apos;s &quot; All Quiet on the Western Front , &quot; rather than comprehensive histories .
LANG_ENG Mr. Englund &apos;s volume joins an unconventional pantheon .
LANG_ENG His book has the most devastating ending I can remember in a piece of nonfiction .
LANG_ENG I won &apos;t give it away .
LANG_ENG But it &apos;s as if he has reached from his book , snatched that dramatis personae page from your fingers , and lighted it with a match .
LANG_ENG After an Earlier Misstep , a Minutely Planned Raid
LANG_ENG Hundreds of police officers were involved , some of them wearing riot helmets .
LANG_ENG The overnight hours of Monday into Tuesday were chosen because Zuccotti Park would be at its emptiest .
LANG_ENG The operation was kept secret from all but a few high-ranking officers , with others initially being told that they were embarking on an exercise .
LANG_ENG Police Commissioner Raymond W. Kelly was at the center , his presence underscoring how the operation was fraught with challenges for the Police Department .
LANG_ENG There could be no repeat of episodes in recent weeks , like the pepper-spraying of protesters , that violated department rules and created a firestorm of public sympathy for the squatters .
LANG_ENG And so the police operation to clear Zuccotti Park of protesters unfolded after two weeks of planning and training .
LANG_ENG Officials had prepared by watching how occupations in other cities played out .
LANG_ENG A major disaster drill was held on Randalls Island , with an eye toward Zuccotti .
LANG_ENG Officials increased so-called disorder training - counterterrorism measures that involve moving large numbers of police officers quickly - to focus on Lower Manhattan .
LANG_ENG The last training session was on Monday night , on the Manhattan side of the East River .
LANG_ENG The orders to move into the park came down at the &quot; last minute , &quot; said someone familiar with the orders , which referred to the assignment only as &quot; an exercise . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; The few cops that I know that were called into this thing , they were not told it was for going into Zuccotti Park , &quot; said the person , who spoke on the condition of anonymity .
LANG_ENG &quot; The only people who were aware of them going into Zuccotti Park were at the very highest levels of the department . &quot;
LANG_ENG One reason for the secrecy was a lesson learned by the city .
LANG_ENG On Oct. 14 , officials wanted to clear the park , but then backed off as hundreds of protesters streamed in ahead of time after hearing of the plans .
LANG_ENG The operation on Tuesday involved officers from various police units , including boroughwide task forces - scores of mobile officers who are usually used to flood high-crime neighborhoods .
LANG_ENG Mr. Kelly said many people , almost like commuters , had been coming and going from the park during the day , making 1 a.m. a good time to move in .
LANG_ENG &quot; It was appropriate to do it when the smallest number of people were in the park , &quot; he said .
LANG_ENG Emergency Service Unit trucks with klieg lights and loudspeakers gathered at Pike Slip and the Franklin D. Roosevelt Drive , near the Manhattan Bridge , before moving out .
LANG_ENG The lights and prerecorded messages booming from the loudspeakers seemed to cow many protesters .
LANG_ENG As the community affairs officers moved into the park in their light-blue windbreakers , many protesters simply gathered their belongings and left .
LANG_ENG No tents were touched until 1: 45 a.m. , the police said , giving the protesters time to gather their belongings .
LANG_ENG Other teams of officers were seen gathering on the perimeter to move in if arrests were needed in the park .
LANG_ENG Reporters in the park were forced to leave .
LANG_ENG Paul J. Browne , the Police Department &apos;s chief spokesman , said it was for their safety .
LANG_ENG But many journalists said that they had been prevented from seeing the police take action in the park , and that they had been roughly handled by officers .
LANG_ENG Mr. Browne said television camera trucks on Church Street , along the park &apos;s western border , were able to capture images .
LANG_ENG As the police moved west through the dense tangle of protesters &quot; personal belongings , including luggage and plastic lawn and leaf bags stuffed with clothing , crews from the Sanitation Department followed , scooping up what was left behind .
LANG_ENG Some protesters who refused to move were dragged out , the images appearing on the Internet soon after .
LANG_ENG A core group of protesters took up positions close to the encampment &apos;s kitchen area , near the center of the park .
LANG_ENG Some made a barrier , and the police moved in to methodically arrest them .
LANG_ENG About 10 people in the epicenter of the encampment locked themselves together by their arms .
LANG_ENG And two people chained themselves to trees , Mr. Kelly said .
LANG_ENG Emergency service officers were called in to cut the locks .
LANG_ENG No arrests were made in the park until about 3: 30 a.m. , Mr. Kelly said .
LANG_ENG The clearing operation was complete about 75 minutes later , the police said .
LANG_ENG Mr. Browne said about 142 people were arrested in the park .
LANG_ENG Most of the arrests were for &quot; disorderly conduct and resisting arrest , &quot; he said .
LANG_ENG The highest-ranking officer on the scene was Joseph J. Esposito , the chief of department , the top uniformed officer in the department .
LANG_ENG Phil T. Pulaski , the department &apos;s chief of detectives , was also there .
LANG_ENG Mr. Kelly , while present , was &quot; not directing operations , &quot; Mr. Browne said .
LANG_ENG A second group of officers waited along the eastern side of Broadway , between Liberty and Cedar Streets , in case they had to move in .
LANG_ENG Barricades were set up at Cortlandt Street , a block north of the park , and at Pine Street , a block south .
LANG_ENG From those positions , the police were seen expanding the perimeter even farther outward from Zuccotti Park .
LANG_ENG At one point , several officers , many with shields , were seen pushing people farther out .
LANG_ENG About 28 people were on the northern perimeter .
LANG_ENG Some of the rowdiest action of the night took place south of the park .
LANG_ENG Around 5 a.m. , south of Pine Street , one protester jumped on the hood of a police car , and others were seen releasing the air from the tires of a police van .
LANG_ENG At one point , a piece of plywood came flying from the crowd .
LANG_ENG In the end , one officer and one protester were hospitalized .

LANG_ENG The union representing hundreds of school support workers who lost their jobs last month plans to sue the city on Wednesday , claiming the layoffs were unnecessary and discriminatory because of their disproportionate impact on schools that serve poor students .
LANG_ENG The lawsuit is possibly the last weapon available to the union , District Council 37 , in its effort to reverse the layoffs .
LANG_ENG Six hundred seventy-two school aides , parent coordinators and other employees who were among the city &apos;s lowest paid lost their jobs on Oct. 7 , in a move union leaders have described as political payback for their refusal to let the city access a health care fund run by labor groups to close its budget gap last spring .
LANG_ENG City officials have categorically denied that accusation .
LANG_ENG At a City Council hearing last month , Schools Chancellor Dennis M. Walcott said the layoffs were a part of the budget they signed off on in June .
LANG_ENG One council member fired back , telling him that nothing in the budget specified that school support staff members would be laid off .
LANG_ENG District Council 37 officials said they filed a notice of claim , warning the Department of Education that it would be sued; it will hold a news conference on Wednesday to announce the lawsuit &apos;s filing formally , in State Supreme Court in Manhattan .
LANG_ENG The suit will list Mr. Walcott and the city &apos;s Department of Education as defendants , and counts eight laid-off workers as plaintiffs , all of them black or Latino women , as were most of the aides who lost their jobs .
LANG_ENG Elizabeth Thomas , a spokeswoman for the city &apos;s Law Department , said the city had not seen the lawsuit and therefore could not comment on its specific allegations .
LANG_ENG According to a draft of the complaint obtained by The New York Times , the union has zeroed in on the number of layoffs at schools in poor parts of the city to support a claim of inequitable treatment , saying poor schools lost more aides because they had been chronically underfinanced .
LANG_ENG It points out that while there were no layoffs on Staten Island and few layoffs in schools in the Upper East Side or TriBeCa , 17 school aides lost their jobs in District 23 in Brownsville , Brooklyn , and 46 were let go in Districts 8 and 9 in the South Bronx .
LANG_ENG Noting the loss of five out of eight school aides employed by Public School 36 in Harlem , where 68 percent of the students live in poverty , the lawsuit says , &quot; This is not a neighborhood of well-heeled parents who can fund-raise to make up the budget shortfalls . &quot;
LANG_ENG P.S. 36 &quot; s budget was cut by 3.26 percent this year , and it also lost money when the city changed the way it calculates the extra financing for each poor student schools enroll .
LANG_ENG The union also claims that high school principals may have been misled by the language in a memo authorizing the dismissal of parent coordinators .
LANG_ENG The memo used the word &quot; excess &quot; to describe the action , even though &quot; excess &quot; more commonly describes the act of moving a teacher from a school &apos;s payroll into a pool of available workers whose salaries are paid by the central office .
LANG_ENG &quot; Sixty-six parent coordinators were laid off , &quot; the draft complaint says , &quot; and not merely excessed . &quot;
LANG_ENG Mission over , Congress ready to agree on Libya
LANG_ENG It finally looks like Republicans and Democrats on Capitol Hill are ready to agree on something related to the uprising in Libya .
LANG_ENG All it took was for the revolt to succeed , longtime dictator Moammar Gaddafi to be captured and killed and American involvement to end .
LANG_ENG On Tuesday , the Senate Foreign Relations Committee is to consider a resolution , sponsored by Chairman John Kerry (D-Mass .), along with Sens. John McCain (R-Ariz .) and Joseph I. Lieberman (I-Conn .), applauding Libyans for their successful rebellion and U.S. troops for their &quot; bravery . &quot;

LANG_ENG The language may sound innocuous , but nothing about the Libya debate has been easy so far .
LANG_ENG This summer , lawmakers from both parties squabbled with each other and the White House over the wording of a series of resolutions to approve or disapprove of the U.S. military &apos;s role in the NATO mission in Libya .
LANG_ENG In June , Kerry and McCain introduced a resolution authorizing the limited use of U.S. military forces in Libya .
LANG_ENG The bill was defeated in the House by a wide bipartisan margin , and it never came up for a vote in the Senate .
LANG_ENG But on the same day that bill failed in the House , the chamber also voted down a measure that would have cut funding for American operations in Libya .

LANG_ENG The Foreign Relations Committee resolution &quot; congratulates the people of Libya for their tremendous courage and extraordinary resilience in liberating themselves &quot; and &quot; commends the men and women of the United States Armed Forces and their coalition partners who engaged in military operations to protect the people of Libya for their extraordinary bravery and professionalism . &quot;
LANG_ENG The resolution also &quot; affirms the national interest of the United States in a successful and irreversible transition to democracy in Libya . &quot;
LANG_ENG Will the new measure earn the full support of the Foreign Relations panel , or will it split lawmakers like the June bill did ?
LANG_ENG The last version was opposed by Sen. Richard G. Lugar (Ind .), the committee &apos;s top Republican .
LANG_ENG Lugar &apos;s spokesman said Monday that he had not seen the final version of the new bill yet .
LANG_ENG Across the Capitol , the House is to vote this week on a constitutional amendment requiring a balanced budget , a top legislative priority of nearly every congressional Republican and some Democrats .
LANG_ENG The measure , sponsored by Rep. Bob Goodlatte (R-Va .), will be the first constitutional amendment on the budget or any other topic to actually get a floor vote in the House or Senate this Congress .
LANG_ENG Other lawmakers have been unsuccessful so far in bringing their amendments to the floor , but not for lack of trying .
LANG_ENG Goodlatte &apos;s is just one of 68 constitutional amendments that have been introduced this Congress , spanning a wide range of topics .
LANG_ENG Some are duplicates - the same bill offered in both the House and Senate - and others are slight variations on each other .
LANG_ENG There are at least 15 versions of the balanced budget amendment in the House alone , and another handful that would &quot; control federal spending . &quot;
LANG_ENG Some of those bills are identical too , but they give their sponsors - particularly freshmen Republicans looking to burnish their resumes - the chance to brag that they &quot; authored &quot; such a measure .
LANG_ENG The Budget Control Act , which passed in August , required that both the House and Senate vote on a balanced budget amendment .
LANG_ENG In March , 58 senators voted in favor of a non-binding resolution supporting the idea of such an amendment .
LANG_ENG Beyond the budget , several members want to limit the number of terms members of Congress can serve , while Rep. José E. Serrano (D-N.Y.) wants to lift the two-term limit for presidents .
LANG_ENG Rep. Jesse L. Jackson Jr . (D-Ill .) has offered a host of amendments guaranteeing the right to quality education and health care , among other subjects .
LANG_ENG An amendment prohibiting same-sex marriage is a perennial offering , as is one prohibiting flag-burning .
LANG_ENG Some lawmakers also want to allow for the nullification of federal laws if they are opposed by two-thirds of the states .
LANG_ENG As eager as members have been to offer amendments , they &apos;re not moving at any faster pace than in past years .
LANG_ENG In the 111th Congress , lawmakers offered 77 such amendments , and they introduced 66 in the 110th .
LANG_ENG The pace pales in comparison to that of the early 1990s , when members regularly offered more than 150 amendments every two years , according to numbers provided by the Senate Library .
LANG_ENG But they weren &apos;t much more successful then than they are now .
LANG_ENG The last amendment to be added - the 27th - was ratified in 1992 , but it was actually proposed by Congress in 1789 .

LANG_ENG Troops feel more pity than respect
LANG_ENG The event was a Wall Street gala that raised millions of dollars for homeless veterans in New York City .

LANG_ENG A senior military officer at the gala , also attended by the Joint Chiefs then-chairman , Adm. Mike Mullen , saw the troops &quot; role differently .
LANG_ENG &quot; They were rolled out like some sort of orphan kid , &quot; the officer wrote in an e-mail .
LANG_ENG I &apos;m sure the organizers meant well .
LANG_ENG I know they did .
LANG_ENG But it wasn &apos;t respect , really . It was pity .
LANG_ENG The starkly conflicting impressions illustrate the uneasy relationship that has taken hold between the military and an often distant , sometimes adoring American public .
LANG_ENG The troops are lavished with praise for their sacrifices .
LANG_ENG But the praise comes with a price , service members say .
LANG_ENG The public increasingly acts as if it feels sorry for those in uniform .
LANG_ENG &quot; We aren &apos;t victims at all , &quot; said Brig. Gen. Sean B. MacFarland , who commanded troops in Iraq and will soon leave for Afghanistan .
LANG_ENG &quot; But it seems that the only way that some can be supportive is to cast us in the role of hapless souls . &quot;
LANG_ENG The topic is a sensitive one for military leaders , who do not want to appear ungrateful or at odds with the public they serve .
LANG_ENG They also realize that the anger that returning troops faced in the latter years of the Vietnam War was far worse .
LANG_ENG As a result , most of the conversations about pity take place quietly and privately among combat veterans .
LANG_ENG After his two sons returned from combat tours with the Marines , retired Col. Mark Cancian warned them that people outside the military would view their service from two perspectives .
LANG_ENG Some would look at them with a sense of awe because they faced down insurgents and traveled to exotic places .
LANG_ENG Others would wonder whether there was an &quot; angry , violent veteran beneath the surface , &quot; said Cancian , who fought in Iraq and returned to a senior government job in Washington .
LANG_ENG During his job search , he said , he sensed that some interviewers had subtly inquired whether he would be able to hold up under the strain of a demanding Washington job immediately after his combat tour .
LANG_ENG &quot; When you talk about your service , you need to counter the negative impressions , &quot; Cancian recalled telling his sons .
LANG_ENG The military &apos;s unease springs , in part , from American indifference to the wars .
LANG_ENG Battlefield achievements are rarely singled out for praise by a country that has little familiarity with the military and sees little direct benefit from the Iraq and Afghanistan wars .
LANG_ENG &quot; We , as a nation , no longer value military heroism in ways that were entirely common in World War II , &quot; said retired Lt. Gen. David Barno , who commanded U.S. troops in Afghanistan .
LANG_ENG Instead , praise from politicians and the public focuses largely on the depth of a service member &apos;s suffering .
LANG_ENG Troops are recognized for the number of tours they have endured , the number of friends they have lost or the extent of their injuries .
LANG_ENG Military Faces Mounting Pressure to Crack Down on Rape
LANG_ENG This week , a landmark hearing will decide whether 28 women and men have a case against the military for alleged inaction on rape .
LANG_ENG If not , hundreds of plaintiffs are lining up for the next one .
LANG_ENG Pfc. Elizabeth Lyman was 25 years old and 11 weeks pregnant the night she says she was raped by a fellow soldier .
LANG_ENG It was October 2008 , and she was heading back to her barracks at Miramar Air Station , just north of San Diego , after dinner with a friend when she ran into a male colleague carrying a 12-pack of beer .
LANG_ENG He asked if he could join her in her room for a drink .
LANG_ENG &quot; A red flag went up , &quot; Lyman says .
LANG_ENG But the Texas native brushed aside her concern .
LANG_ENG I thought , no , he &apos;s 19 .
LANG_ENG &quot; He &apos;s from Texas , &quot; she says .
LANG_ENG &quot; I wish I had listened to my instincts . &quot;
LANG_ENG Within minutes of entering Lyman &apos;s room , the man came up from behind her , throwing her down and raping her , she alleges .
LANG_ENG This week , on Nov. 18 , Lyman and 27 other current and former military personnel will anxiously await a court ruling in a historic hearing to be held in Arlington , Va . , that will decide whether a suit they have brought against former defense secretary Robert Gates and his predecessor , Donald Rumsfeld , will go to trial .
LANG_ENG The suit alleges that Gates and Rumsfeld failed to curtail widespread rape within the military , in violation of soldiers &quot; constitutional rights .
LANG_ENG Filed in February by Susan Burke , a Washington , D.C. , attorney , the suit initially involved 17 plaintiffs but grew to 28 in the following months - 25 women and three men , all of whom allege that they were raped or sexually assaulted by fellow soldiers , and that the military failed to investigate , prosecute , or provide adequate justice after the alleged attacks .
LANG_ENG Burke says she is going after the military &apos;s top brass because the problem begins with them .
LANG_ENG &quot; The military is a top-down structure , &quot; she says .
LANG_ENG &quot; Who are the individuals who are in the position to eradicate the culture of retaliation ? The top leadership . &quot;
LANG_ENG Burke also says that since February , she has been contacted by nearly 400 other survivors , many of whom could be part of future suits .
LANG_ENG Her strategy: rather than piling all the plaintiffs into this case - putting all her eggs in one basket , so to speak - she will file multiple cases , if need be , to keep the pressure on .
LANG_ENG The defense team for the Department of Defense , led by United States Attorney Neil MacBride , has filed a motion to dismiss the case .
LANG_ENG The court papers , obtained by Newsweek , detail the defense strategy - essentially , that the military cannot be sued by current or former soldiers for injuries incurred in the armed forces .
LANG_ENG Per a 1950 Supreme Court ruling called the Feres Doctrine , the federal government is not liable for injury sustained by active-duty personnel .
LANG_ENG &quot; The alleged harms are incident to plaintiffs &quot; military service , &quot; the documents say .
LANG_ENG The Department of Defense declined to comment for this story .
LANG_ENG Things have changed since the &apos; 50s .
LANG_ENG While women are still officially prohibited from serving in combat , that distinction is in name only: modern wars rarely have clear front lines .
LANG_ENG In both Iraq and Afghanistan , teams of female soldiers are playing increasingly important roles , especially in reaching out to local women in war zones .
LANG_ENG The result: between those two wars , 150 female soldiers have died - two thirds of them during combat situations .
LANG_ENG It &apos;s time for the military to modernize as well , Burke says .
LANG_ENG She isn &apos;t alone in her fight; three other lawsuits are under way in addition to hers .
LANG_ENG At Yale Law School , the Veterans &quot; Legal Services Clinic is preparing a case against the four major military academies for allegedly fostering a misogynistic atmosphere .
LANG_ENG Separately , the nonprofit Vietnam Veterans of America is assembling a suit against the military that focuses on the &quot; personality disorder &quot; diagnoses assigned to rape victims in order to discharge them from service - a common occurrence , according to activists such as Anu Bhagwati of the Service Women &apos;s Action Network , a human-rights group .
LANG_ENG Her group filed a suit in December against the Veterans Administration , charging it with discriminatory practices in the way it handles benefit claims for people who say they were sexually assaulted in the service .
LANG_ENG Nine separate bills have been introduced in Congress by a bipartisan mix of senators and representatives , proposing a range of fixes .
LANG_ENG In 2010 there were 3,158 sexual assaults reported , according to the Department of Defense &apos;s Sexual Assault Prevention and Response Office .
LANG_ENG But assaults are notoriously underreported , and by the Pentagon &apos;s own estimate , that figure accounts for approximately 13.5 percent of the estimated 19,000 incidents that occurred that year .
LANG_ENG The report , released in March , also examines prosecution rates: in 2010 , 20 percent of reported cases in the military went to trial - half the rate of the civilian justice system .
LANG_ENG The plaintiffs in Burke &apos;s case describe their attacks as just the first of a series of traumas .
LANG_ENG In Lyman &apos;s case , she reported the alleged rape to military police less than an hour after it occurred .
LANG_ENG That night , she says , she was asked to recount the details of her rape 11 separate times to a stream of police , doctors , and commanders .
LANG_ENG She did a rape kit , and her assailant &apos;s blood - from a cut on his arm - was found in her bed .
LANG_ENG Six months later , in April 2009 , Lyman sat through a hearing before a judge , during which she was questioned about having had sex with her boyfriend earlier on the day of the attack .
LANG_ENG Six people testified as character witnesses on behalf of the alleged perpetrator , who was eventually acquitted .
LANG_ENG &quot; I remember the day when the verdict was read , &quot; Lyman says .
LANG_ENG &quot; I thought I was going to go into labor - I ran screaming from the courtroom . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; They put me into the psychiatric unit , and when I got out , I remember saying to my command officer , &quot; That wasn &apos;t his trial . That was my trial . &quot; &quot;
LANG_ENG Lyman was ordered to visit a military psychiatrist , who eventually diagnosed her with a personality disorder , rendering her unfit for service .
LANG_ENG In January 2010 she was discharged under less-than-honorable terms , preventing her from receiving any benefits .
LANG_ENG As for whether the case filed by Lyman and her fellow plaintiffs moves forward , it &apos;s a long shot , says John Turley , professor of law at George Washington University Law School .
LANG_ENG However , he notes , &quot; In my view they should be able to move forward . &quot;
LANG_ENG What stands between them and a verdict is this doctrine that has been criticized since it was first issued .
LANG_ENG I &apos;ve been a lifelong critic of the Feres Doctrine .
LANG_ENG The military is decades behind because they don &apos;t have the same incentive and deterrent posed by liability .
LANG_ENG He adds , &quot; The odds are heavily against them ... but it &apos;s important for them to try . &quot;
LANG_ENG These things only change when good people are willing to fight .
LANG_ENG If the hearing doesn &apos;t go their way , Burke , the plaintiffs &quot; lawyer , will cue up her hundreds of other plaintiffs for future cases .
LANG_ENG &quot; We will continue battling the military on this issue until reform occurs , &quot; she says .
LANG_ENG Or until we die , whichever comes first .
LANG_ENG Walls Have Eyes: How Researchers Are Studying You on Facebook
LANG_ENG Before he became the new face of right-wing extremism in Europe , Anders Behring Breivik was just another guy airing his anti-immigration views online .
LANG_ENG On Monday , Breivik , who admitted to a killing spree in Norway in July which left 77 people dead , faced his first public court hearing .
LANG_ENG While Breivik may have acted alone , he was far from alone in cyberspace: he had spent much of the time leading up to his attack at his computer , chatting with some of the millions of nationalists who support right-wing groups on social-networking sites .
LANG_ENG After this summer &apos;s tragedy , researchers wanted to find out more about these people .
LANG_ENG But how to find them ?
LANG_ENG Easy - just log on to Facebook .
LANG_ENG &quot; We realized that it wasn &apos;t that difficult to get to them at all , &quot; says Jamie Bartlett , the lead author of a recently published report on European digital populism by British think tank DEMOS .
LANG_ENG Facebook &apos;s stash of personal information is so encyclopedic , says Bartlett , that the researchers could simply use the site &apos;s advertising tool to pinpoint their desired demographic with scientific accuracy - the way marketers have been doing for years .
LANG_ENG Bartlett &apos;s team found half a million fans of right-wing groups across Europe and then targeted them with ads , but instead of linking to a new band or diet product , the ads invited users to complete a survey that asked questions about their education level , attitudes towards violence and optimism about their own future .
LANG_ENG By reaching out through Facebook , Bartlett and his colleagues were able to survey over 10,000 followers of 14 far-right parties in 11 European countries - all without ever leaving the office .
LANG_ENG &quot; It &apos;s quite a new way of doing research , &quot; Bartlett says .
LANG_ENG Some of the results aren &apos;t all that surprising: online supporters of right-wing groups tend to be young , male , and vexed by immigration .
LANG_ENG But in something of a twist , &quot; those who combine their online activism with offline activism are more reasonable , more democratic and less violent than those who just remain behind the computer screen . &quot;
LANG_ENG DEMOS &apos;s work is just one example of how Facebook is becoming a hot new tool in the hands of scientists .
LANG_ENG Think tanks , medical researchers and political scientists are using the site to study everything from health issues to social trends as expressed in &apos; Likes &apos; , wall posts and status updates .
LANG_ENG With over 800 million active users adding an average of three pieces of content per day , the Facebook &quot; data supernova &quot; is generating a research boom , driving the number of academic papers with the site &apos;s name in the title up almost 800% over the past five years .
LANG_ENG For some researchers , the beauty of Facebook is that it lets you study people you couldn &apos;t approach with a clipboard on the street .
LANG_ENG &quot; Before , when you tried to survey people from the British National Party , it would be very difficult to identify them . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; You &apos;d have to go through the party and they would never allow you access , &quot; says Bartlett .
LANG_ENG &quot; Facebook cuts all that out - you go to them directly . &quot;
LANG_ENG Others say the site could offer a way to identify and tackle social-health problems .
LANG_ENG A recent study by Dr. Megan Moreno of the University of Wisconsin , Madison and her colleagues found that undergraduates who discussed their drunken exploits on Facebook were significantly more at risk for problem drinking than students who were silent on the topic .
LANG_ENG Moreno suggests that students &apos; peers , such as residential advisors , could monitor the site and intervene to help a student who posts one too many boozy status updates .
LANG_ENG &quot; You can &apos;t treat a problem if you can &apos;t diagnose it , &quot; Dimitri Christakis , Moreno &apos;s co-author and director of the Center for Child Health , Behavior and Development at the Seattle Children &apos;s Research Institute , told the Washington Post .
LANG_ENG &quot; We &apos;ve found a way to identify kids at risk who would not otherwise be diagnosed . &quot;
LANG_ENG Facebook , too , is constantly tapping into its own population for data .
LANG_ENG The site &apos;s Data Team has worked up stats on relationship status and Valentine &apos;s Day , voter turnout in the 2010 midterms , and a National Happiness Index (mainly using information only it can access - champions of digital freedom have long held that the site should make its vast trove of data available to others for research .) 
LANG_ENG But Facebook &apos;s users know the site is watching them , whether they like it or not - the trade off for being able to play Scrabble with a friend thousands of miles away is allowing the site to mine your personal information .
LANG_ENG But what about when Facebook users become part of a survey they don &apos;t even know about ?
LANG_ENG While researchers have well-defined guidelines about how they collect their data offline , online it &apos;s a free-for-all .
LANG_ENG &quot; I don &apos;t think a lot of users had even thought about the fact that a researcher could be looking at their profiles , &quot; says Neil Selwyn , a sociologist at the London Knowledge Lab , of a 2006 study he conducted using students &apos; public Facebook walls .
LANG_ENG &quot; As far as they were concerned , it was just between them and their friends . &quot;
LANG_ENG Then there &apos;s the question of methodology .
LANG_ENG Even offline , there &apos;s no guarantee that a research subject is being completely honest .
LANG_ENG On Facebook , it &apos;s impossible to know how much of a user &apos;s profile information and wall posts are true .
LANG_ENG &quot; What you say on Facebook and what you do outside of Facebook are two completely different things , &quot; says Selwyn .
LANG_ENG Which is why , despite his years as a technology researcher , Selwyn thinks a clipboard and a pen are still the best research tools anyone can use .
LANG_ENG &quot; There &apos;s no substitute for going into the real world and speaking to real people , &quot; he says .
LANG_ENG &quot; Social research is supposed to be about the social - and a hell of a lot of the social still takes place offline . &quot;

LANG_ENG 7 Lessons From the Lost Steve Jobs Interview , Showing at a Theater This Week
LANG_ENG Following the death of Steve Jobs on Oct. 5 , there were some who compared him to Henry Ford - singling out the tech genius as the Great Modern Inventor .
LANG_ENG But what Apple fans might find most revealing in a recently rediscovered Jobs interview dusted off in advance of special movie screenings in major markets this week , is how much this creative mind understood about business processes and product workflows .
LANG_ENG He was certainly a designer , dreamer and self-identified hippie , but he was also a meticulous organizer and flow-chart tweaker - a man who believed that many business executives suffered from &quot; a disease of thinking that a really great idea is 90 percent of the work ... &#91; but &#93; there &apos;s a tremendous amount of craftsmanship between a great idea and a great product . &quot;
LANG_ENG Back in 1995 , Bob Cringely was developing the TV series Triumph of the Nerds about the dawn of the personal computer , and he sat down for more than an hour with Jobs for a rare , extended conversation .
LANG_ENG At that point in time it had been roughly 10 years since Jobs had been forced out of Apple , and he was already hard at work at his new computer company NeXT , looking forward to the popular adoption of the Internet .
LANG_ENG While a small portion of Cringely &apos;s interview was used in Nerds , he says that the interview &apos;s master copies went missing in shipping .
LANG_ENG It was only following Jobs &quot; death that a complete VHS copy was discovered in the director &apos;s garage .
LANG_ENG This footage , slightly re-edited , is the basis of the 68-minute Steve Jobs: The Lost Interview , coming to Landmark Theaters this Wednesday and Thursday .
LANG_ENG For someone known to loathe in-depth interviews , Jobs seems surprisingly eager here to expound on his technological philosophies and business strategies .
LANG_ENG And incredibly thoughtful .
LANG_ENG At four different points during the interview , there are major gaps between questions and answers - 10 to 15-second ruminations , where Jobs is clearly weighing his thoughts , aiming for precision .
LANG_ENG There are also moments where Jobs &quot; perfectionism rises to the surface , where he seems impatient about Cringely &apos;s questions , nudging him to move faster towards issues or subplots of greater import .
LANG_ENG Filmed before the era of Pixar , the iPod or the iPhone , there &apos;s something eerily prescient about the Lost Interview , as Jobs assesses what he sees other companies doing right and wrong , and offers his own vision of the future of computing .
LANG_ENG It &apos;s one thing to read posthumous appraisals of Jobs &quot; career , but there &apos;s something raw and inspiring about the Jobs we see here , the anxious dreamer on the brink of greatness .
LANG_ENG He has a vision for the way things could be and should be .
LANG_ENG Not long after spelling it all out here he went and did it .
LANG_ENG A quick rundown of seven notable Lost Interview sequences:
LANG_ENG On how he learned to run a business:
LANG_ENG &quot; Throughout the years in business , I &apos;d always ask: &quot; Why do you do things ? &quot; and the answers you invariably get are: &quot; Oh , that &apos;s just the way it &apos;s done . &quot; &quot;
LANG_ENG Nobody knows why they do what they do , nobody thinks about things very deeply in business .
LANG_ENG That &apos;s what I found .
LANG_ENG Jobs goes on to detail his efforts to streamline accounting at Apple .
LANG_ENG Baffled by the way costs were recorded - often beginning with a &quot; standard cost &quot; that amounted to a blind guess , which was then adjusted with a &quot; variance &quot; - he developed an automated factory that ensured they could determine business costs down to the second .
LANG_ENG On pranking the Pope:
LANG_ENG Jobs recounts the &quot; Blue Box &quot; that he built with Steve Wozniak - a device that allowed people to effectively hack the phone company and make long distance calls for free .
LANG_ENG The device has been widely written about before , but Jobs &quot; euphoria here in describing the Blue Box as an act of empowerment is infectious .
LANG_ENG He describes how he and Wozniak would test the box by using a pay phone , placing a call , and then connecting from one AT &amp; T network to another while looping in as many satellites as possible .
LANG_ENG &quot; We were wrapping things around the globe a half dozen times and you would yell through the pay phone and it would come through a minute later in the pay phone next door , &quot; Jobs says with a giggle .
LANG_ENG &quot; We were young and what we learned was that we could build something ourselves that could control billions of dollars of infrastructure around the world . &quot;
LANG_ENG Us two , we didn &apos;t know much , but we could build a little thing that could control a giant thing - it was an incredible lesson , and I don &apos;t think there would have ever been Apple without it .
LANG_ENG Jobs goes on to detail one glorious prank that he and Wozniak nearly pulled of , ringing the Vatican via the Blue Box in the middle of the night and requesting to speak to the Pope , while doing their very best impression of Henry Kissinger .
LANG_ENG As various members of the Catholic hierarchy were summoned in the middle of the night to talk to the American diplomat , the two burst into giggles just before the Pope himself was roused to come answer the phone .
LANG_ENG On falling in love with technology (and cold-calling Bill Hewlett):
LANG_ENG At the age of 12 , on the hunt for spare parts to build a frequency counter , he looked up Bill Hewlett in the phone book and gave him a call .
LANG_ENG Not long after their 20-minute phone conversation , Jobs landed a part-time summer job at Hewlett-Packard .
LANG_ENG &quot; That made a remarkable influence on me , it was the only company I had seen at that age and it formed my view of what a company was and how well they treated their employees . &quot;
LANG_ENG Jobs later became one of the HP employees to visit the company &apos;s Palo Alto research labs , where he saw &quot; the first desktop computer ever made . &quot;
LANG_ENG It was as big as a suitcase , had a small cathode ray tube display , and I fell in love with it .
LANG_ENG I would get a ride up to HP as a teenager and hang around that machine and write programs for it .
LANG_ENG On improvising innovation:
LANG_ENG At several points in the interview , Jobs talks about inventing new products on the fly .
LANG_ENG Early in his career , as he set out to sell a handful of circuit boards , he was asked by a customer to assemble the full computer .
LANG_ENG Working on only 30 days of credit , he had to figure out both the assembly and delivery of the finished devices .
LANG_ENG Later while at Apple , he recalls the struggles he faced in developing a computer mouse:
LANG_ENG &quot; I remember having dramatic arguments ... they were screaming at me that it would take five years &#91; to build a mouse that would cost $ 300 &#93; , and I got fed up and went outside and found a designer . &quot;
LANG_ENG Ninety days later , we had a mouse we could build for $ 15 that was incredibly reliable .
LANG_ENG On great companies losing steam:
LANG_ENG Long before he led the revival of Apple , Jobs presciently foresaw the ways in which so many industry leaders would stumble in their vision , and lose control of their market share .
LANG_ENG &quot; Say you work at IBM or Xerox , so you make a better copier or printer , so what ? &quot;
LANG_ENG You have a monopoly of the market share , so the company &apos;s not more successful .
LANG_ENG Sales and marketing makes it more successful , so &#91; it &apos;s those kinds of people &#93; who end up running companies and the product people get driven out of the decision-making forum .
LANG_ENG The product genius that led to that monopolistic position is rotted out by people who have no conception of good products vs. bad products - the craftsmanship required .
LANG_ENG That &apos;s what happened at Xerox ...
LANG_ENG Xerox could have owned the entire computer industry .
LANG_ENG It could have been ten times its size , could have been the Microsoft of the 90s ...
LANG_ENG They grabbed defeat from victory .
LANG_ENG On innovation as art form:
LANG_ENG &quot; There &apos;s a tremendous amount of craftsmanship between a great idea and a great product ... as you evolve that great idea , it changes and grows . &quot;
LANG_ENG You learn a lot about the subtleties of it .
LANG_ENG There are tradeoffs you have to make - certain things you can &apos;t make electrons do , glass do , robots do , factories do .
LANG_ENG You have to keep 5,000 things in your brain - these concepts - fitting them all together ...
LANG_ENG Ultimately it comes down to taste - it comes down to trying to expose yourself to the best stuff humans have done , and then trying to bring those things in to what you &apos;re doing .
LANG_ENG Picasso said that good artists copy , great artists steal .
LANG_ENG We &apos;ve always been shameless about stealing great ideas .
LANG_ENG It &apos;s part of what made the Macintosh great , was that the people who were working on it were musicians , poets , artists , zoologists , historians who just happened to be the best computer scientists in the world .
LANG_ENG If not for computer science , they would be doing amazing things in other fields .

LANG_ENG And Thomas &quot; wife , Ginni , is a high-profile conservative activist .
LANG_ENG Moreover , conservatives argue that it &apos;s Justice Elena Kagan who has an ethical issue , not Scalia and Thomas .
LANG_ENG Kagan served as solicitor general in the Obama administration when the first legal challenges to the law were brought at the trial court level .
LANG_ENG Her critics have pushed for Kagan to recuse herself from hearing the case , saying that she was too invested in defending the law then to be impartial now .

LANG_ENG Dire warnings from Pentagon over potential defense cuts

LANG_ENG The Pentagon already is digesting $ 450 billion of reductions over the next decade but now fears an additional $ 600 billion or more in cuts may be imminent if Congress cannot reach a deal on spending .
LANG_ENG &quot; The impacts of these cuts would be devastating for the department , &quot; Panetta said in a letter to Sens. John McCain , R-Arizona , and Lindsey Graham , R-South Carolina .

LANG_ENG &quot; Such a large cut , applied in this indiscriminate manner , would render most of our ship and construction projects unexecutable -- you cannot buy three quarters of a ship or a building -- and seriously damage other modernization efforts , &quot; Panetta wrote to the senators .
LANG_ENG &quot; We would also be forced to separate many of our civilian personnel involuntarily and , because the reduction would be imposed so quickly , we would almost certainly have to furlough civilians in order to meet the target . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; These changes would break faith with those who maintain our military and seriously damage readiness . &quot;
LANG_ENG The cuts would eventually hit combat troops , Panetta said .

LANG_ENG &quot; Contracting personnel would be cut , resulting in delays in the contracts and the contract oversight that support the war . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; Payroll personnel would be cut , resulting in late payments to wartime vendors , and legal and policy support would be disrupted . &quot;
LANG_ENG The two senators had written Panetta 10 days ago , asking for details of the potential impact of the automatic cuts on the Defense Department .
LANG_ENG &quot; The consequence of a sequester on the Defense Department would set off a swift decline of the United States as the world &apos;s leading military power . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; We are staunchly opposed to this draconian action , &quot; the senators said in a joint statement Monday afternoon when they released Panetta &apos;s letter .
LANG_ENG &quot; This is not an outcome that we can live with , and it is certainly not one that we should impose on ourselves . &quot;
LANG_ENG &quot; The sequester is a threat to the national security interests of the United States , and it should not be allowed to occur . &quot;
LANG_ENG Whether the Panetta letter and the fresh warnings from Senators will increase pressure for a budget compromise or step up calls to exempt the Pentagon from cuts remains to be seen .
LANG_ENG Panetta has been increasingly outspoken about the possible cuts , although he came to the top Pentagon job with years of budget expertise himself in Congress and the White House and knowing that he was facing tough choices .
LANG_ENG At a news conference last week , the secretary of defense painted a bleak picture of what could lie ahead -- a military with a shell but no core .

LANG_ENG How to spot a lie
LANG_ENG A glance at recent headlines indicates just how serious and pervasive deceit and lying are in daily life .
LANG_ENG Republican presidential candidate Herman Cain is busy trading allegations of sexual harassment with several women; each side accuses the other of lying .
LANG_ENG Administrators at Penn State have been charged with perjury for allegedly covering up reports that a retired football coach was sexually assaulting boys .
LANG_ENG Last week French president Nicolas Sarkozy was caught on an open microphone asserting to U.S. President Barack Obama that Israeli Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu is a liar .
LANG_ENG Lying has destroyed careers and convulsed countries .
LANG_ENG New York congressman and Internet flasher Anthony Weiner made a fool of himself issuing denials quickly contradicted by incontrovertible evidence .
LANG_ENG Former presidential candidate John Edwards has been charged with campaign finance violations connected to the cover-up of an extramarital affair .
LANG_ENG And then again , no one who lived through it will ever forget the media circus President Bill Clinton unleashed by lying during his second term in office about his sexual involvement with Monica Lewinsky .
LANG_ENG Tales of cheating on school and college tests are rife .
LANG_ENG There have been instances where teachers have given students test answers in order to make themselves look good on their performance reviews .
LANG_ENG Mentors who should be teaching the opposite are sending a message that lying and cheating are acceptable .
LANG_ENG How much deceit do we encounter ?
LANG_ENG On a given day , studies show , you may be lied to anywhere from 10 to 200 times .
LANG_ENG Now granted , many of those are white lies .
LANG_ENG Another study showed that strangers lied three times within the first 10 minutes of meeting each other .
LANG_ENG Detecting lies , or &quot; lie spotting , &quot; is an essential skill for everyone to acquire , for both personal and professional reasons .
LANG_ENG Far from being a parlor game similar to , say , charades , where the object is to exclaim , &quot; Gotcha , &quot; deception detection is a serious branch of knowledge based on scientific data collected over the last six decades at prestigious universities conducting in-depth research projects , especially in psychology and physiology .
LANG_ENG One result of the research is that most old myths about lying have been debunked .
LANG_ENG Liars do look you in the eye .
LANG_ENG They do not always stutter , stammer , blush or fidget .
LANG_ENG Don &apos;t conclude from this that liars are hard to spot and difficult to unmask .
LANG_ENG A trained lie spotter can get to the truth by learning about statement structure , facial micro-expressions , question formation and timing .
LANG_ENG I spent several years surveying the scientific findings in the vast and ever emerging body of knowledge on deception , and it is clear that deception detection is a modern skill that is easy to learn and helpful in navigating our complex world -- especially if your professional responsibilities include hiring , interviewing , negotiating or managing .
LANG_ENG Good liars are skilled at reading others well , putting them at ease , managing their own emotions and intuitively sensing how others perceive them .
LANG_ENG We know from research that extroverts lie more than introverts , that men tell more &quot; self-oriented &quot; lies while women tell more &quot; other-oriented &quot; lies -- usually to protect someone &apos;s feelings -- that married people lie less frequently to their partners than unmarried people do (but the lies they do tell tend to be &quot; whoppers &quot;).
LANG_ENG We also know that if you are perceived as a wrongdoer , others will feel less guilt in lying to you .
LANG_ENG How do you tell if someone is lying ?
LANG_ENG First , observe your subject &apos;s normal behavior .
LANG_ENG This is called &quot; baselining . &quot;
LANG_ENG It helps provide a reference point for measuring changes later .
LANG_ENG Observe your subject &apos;s posture , laugh , vocal quality .
LANG_ENG You &apos;d better know if someone normally taps their foot all the time so you don &apos;t make unjust accusations when you see foot-tapping in the middle of the meeting .
LANG_ENG Then look for clusters of deceptive verbal and nonverbal behaviors .
LANG_ENG Consider these clusters red flags , not proof of deception .
LANG_ENG Deceptive people might freeze their upper body when trying to remember their story , they might point their feet toward the door , lean toward an exit , shift their posture in significant ways or exhibit &quot; post-interview relief &quot; -- that exaggerated exhale of relief and shift in posture when all the hard questions are over .
LANG_ENG Interrogators often falsely signal that an interview is over just to look for that post-interview relief .
LANG_ENG Also , pay attention to your subject &apos;s language .
LANG_ENG Scott Peterson famously slipped and used the past tense while claiming his murdered wife was alive , launching a nationwide search for her .
LANG_ENG Deceptive individuals might also use distancing language: &quot; I did not have sexual relations with that woman ... Miss Lewinsky &quot; or repeat a hard question in its entirety .
LANG_ENG The most common verbal indicators are subtle .
LANG_ENG Someone might use lots of &quot; qualifying language &quot; when answering a hard question: &quot; Well ... to tell you the truth ... as far as I know ... to the best of my knowledge . &quot;
LANG_ENG This renders the answer perceptual rather than factual and is often a red flag .
LANG_ENG There &apos;s no magic bullet for detecting lies , but developing skills to ferret out deception is possible .
LANG_ENG These skills will enhance anyone &apos;s chances of avoiding victimization by scam artists in their professional and personal lives .
